<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10204862639145955775">Winnie and Helen have kept each others worst secrets for more than fifty years. Now, because she believes she is dying, Helen wants to expose everything. And Winnie angrily determines that she must be the one to tell her daughter, Pearl, about the past—including the terrible truth even Helen does not know. And so begins Winnie's story of her life on a small island outside</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10204862639145955775">Winnie and Helen have kept each others worst secrets for more than fifty years. Now, because she believes she is dying, Helen wants to expose everything. And Winnie angrily determines that she must be the one to tell her daughter, Pearl, about the past—including the terrible truth even Helen does not know. And so begins Winnie's story of her life on a small island outside Shanghai in the 1920s, and other places in China during World War II, and traces the happy and desperate events that led to Winnie's coming to America in 1949.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_564213816">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/564213816" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4529669-willow" class="left imgcol" title="Willow "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1447206325p2/4529669.jpg" alt="Willow " /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/564213816?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 27, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4529669-willow" class="user" title="Willow ">Willow </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1651580.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780679748083" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=china" class="actionLinkLite">china</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer564213816">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12608007897396686255">Amy Tan writes about women (complex women!) and I think that’s one of the things I love about her books. The men in her stories are shadows, almost undeveloped, with little presence except when they are cruel and threatening. <br /><br />I found this closed women’s world wonderfully refreshing, especially after reading so many books where men are the main focus. In <em>The Godfather</em>, Mario Puzo jumped into Mama Corleone’s point of view for just one small bit; just long enough to reveal that the wife of the mafi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12608007897396686255">Amy Tan writes about women (complex women!) and I think that’s one of the things I love about her books. The men in her stories are shadows, almost undeveloped, with little presence except when they are cruel and threatening. <br /><br />I found this closed women’s world wonderfully refreshing, especially after reading so many books where men are the main focus. In <em>The Godfather</em>, Mario Puzo jumped into Mama Corleone’s point of view for just one small bit; just long enough to reveal that the wife of the mafia godfather did not concern herself with her husband’s violent world. She didn’t care. After all, men never concerned themselves with women’s problems. They were from two different worlds, and this separate view reminded me of Winnie, the main protagonist in <em>The Kitchen God’s Wife</em>. Winnie is so distant from her cruel husband that she doesn’t even know if Wen Fu is a gangster, and Tan never confirms it either. After all, it’s not important. The main focus is all about women. <br /><br />And the women are vivid too. Winnie and Helen come alive. By the end of the book I felt I knew them… quite well. Both their personalities and voices are so strong. I can still imagine them bickering with each other. <br />And they are friends too -- true friends, who resent and care about each other. They even talk trash, yet they still stick together. I found this push and pull so real. There’s always a bit of one-upmanship with friends, and Tan knows this. You want good things for your friends, but you never want them to be <strong>too</strong> successful or <strong>too</strong> happy. It’s like sibling rivalry. LOL<br /><br />Another great character was Auntie Du, who is an older woman with no husband (he died) and no money. She can’t even write. But she turns out to be this lovely hero, whom I just wanted to hug. In a society that undervalues widows and spinsters, I loved that she saved the day, and she didn’t even ask for credit. What a great character! <br /><br />As with all of Tan’s books, I love her simple but lyrical prose, and I love all the details she adds about China before and after the war. Tan takes you a different world and a different culture, but makes it familiar simply by introducing you to these fascinating and flawed women. The love and pain they feel is universal, and I found myself quite choked up at the end, thinking about the friends and family that are in my own women’s world. <br /><br />Problems I had with this book were the slow parts. Winnie’s daughter Pearl is definitely not as interesting as her mom (although I found her relationship with her mother poignant.) Wen Fu (Winnie’s husband) is almost too cruel, too inhuman. He’s such a monster. I really hated him, which is good for a villain. But like all Tan’s men, he was a shadow, very evil, but a shadow nevertheless. I think Tan revealed way too much of the ending in the beginning when Pearl is telling her story. Having Winnie go back and explain how things led up to where she was in the present, when you already know the outcome, kills a lot of the suspense. <br /><br />But all in all, I enjoyed this a lot. If you’ve never read a Tan book, you’ll be instantly transported to a new world. But if you’ve read her other books, you’ll definitely recognize many of the same themes and character types that she usually writes about. <br /><br />I give <em>The Kitchen’s God’s Wife</em> **** ½. <br /><br />It’s a great book!<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12608007897396686255">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_359035000">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/359035000" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6257531-caroline" class="left imgcol" title="Caroline"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1329765864p2/6257531.jpg" alt="Caroline" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/359035000?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6257531-caroline" class="user" title="Caroline">Caroline</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6257531-caroline?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer359035000">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12632793086631252872">The book starts out in contemporary America, and is narrated by Pearl – a second generation Chinese emigrant, who is trying to balance her own 21st century American family life with the needs of her Chinese mother and her mother’s friends. From the third chapter on the narrating is taken over by Winnie, Pearl’s mother, and it transforms into being the story of her life – told against the background of her living in Shanghai in the 1930s and 40s, under the Kuomintang, but with the Communists alre</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12632793086631252872">The book starts out in contemporary America, and is narrated by Pearl – a second generation Chinese emigrant, who is trying to balance her own 21st century American family life with the needs of her Chinese mother and her mother’s friends. From the third chapter on the narrating is taken over by Winnie, Pearl’s mother, and it transforms into being the story of her life – told against the background of her living in Shanghai in the 1930s and 40s, under the Kuomintang, but with the Communists already making their presence felt, and her experiences with the Japanese invasion of Shanghai . On a personal level it is about Winnie’s relationships, with her own mother, with her first husband Wen Fu, with her friends Hulan, Peanut, Min and Grand Auntie Du, and finally, about her relationship with the Chinese-American translator Jimmy Louie. <br /><br />The first pillar of the book for me was the suffocating nature of the family life portrayed. It starts in the present day, with a mother/daughter relationship that I found cringingly intrusive. In fact the whole family is way too over bearing, and that isn’t even just blood relatives. It also includes various friends who have been sucked into this gooey familial web. <br /><br />The second pillar was the superstition that is rife throughout the whole story. From the eccentricities of Grand Auntie Du’s household alter , to a plethora of omens that drench the lives of these people – inspiring or damning – and always taken seriously. At one stage there is a visit to a fortune teller, but this seems superfluous given that their lives are already heavily dominated by a super-awareness of portents of good and bad fortune.<br /><br />The third and final pillar of the book for me was the ubiquitous male dominance in Chinese society at this time. This was illustrated in the general culture, in the licence given to Winnie’s first husband, and in the heavy governance of her father, both towards her and her mother. Men ruled and marriage was all. But this pillar had woodworm.... The women in the story were rebelling – through friendship and support for one another, through the changing politics of the time, and through the sheer cussedness and determination of the main female protagonists in the story.<br /><br />My least favourite character at the beginning of the book was Helen, or Hulen as she was known in China, but as time went on I could not help but be beguiled by the riches that the author brought to her personality. She was stupid and clumsy, touching, wily, endearing, maddening, loyal, naive, preposterous, turncoat and vulnerable... By the end of the book I found her quite irresistible. Such a huge character. It was a pleasure to follow the ups and downs of her friendship with Winnie, and have my initial dislike so overturned. In fact all the women characters in the book had a lot of presence for me.<br /><br />The book ends with a return to the present day, with Winnie and her daughter Pearl coming to terms with a lot of things in one another’s lives that they had previously kept secret, and we sense a new closeness between them.<br /><br />For the most part though the book is harsh and quite brutal. Winnie did not have an easy life, not only in personal terms, but also in what she went through whilst being bombed, in fleeing the Japanese army, and in the aftermath of the war. Having said that – there is a happy ending – and the glow from that did much to warm the cockles of my cowardly heart.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12632793086631252872">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer42139304">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9068355806933823464">Maybe its because I just finished it, but I really liked this book. This is a story of a Chinese woman named "Winnie" and the secrets she keeps from her daughter, not only to protect her daughter, but to protect herself and her best friend. As with many of the books we read, Winnie has had a hard life, almost horrific in some respects but the reason I love her is that the story isn't tragic, she doesn't complain about it (too much), or make herself out to be a hero, well except maybe in her own</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9068355806933823464">Maybe its because I just finished it, but I really liked this book. This is a story of a Chinese woman named "Winnie" and the secrets she keeps from her daughter, not only to protect her daughter, but to protect herself and her best friend. As with many of the books we read, Winnie has had a hard life, almost horrific in some respects but the reason I love her is that the story isn't tragic, she doesn't complain about it (too much), or make herself out to be a hero, well except maybe in her own mind. She tells her story to her daughter and it is as if I would tell something to my best friend, there are some embellishments, side gossip stories, and a little humor. The relationship between Winnie and her best friend Hulan/Helen is so realistic it has to make you laugh a little inside. The things they go through, the love/hate relationship, its like a dramatic or disfunctional version of I love Lucy, its makes it so much easier to connect to these characters and a much more enjoyable read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9068355806933823464">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1195759937">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195759937" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange" class="left imgcol" title="Bloodorange"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1386872100p2/23066833.jpg" alt="Bloodorange" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1195759937?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 24, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange" class="user" title="Bloodorange">Bloodorange</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=us" class="actionLinkLite">us</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=library" class="actionLinkLite">library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1195759937">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4856634951032306092">I officially do not want to read anything by Tan again. At least this is how I feel at the moment.<br /><br />Why the three stars: <em>The Kitchen God's Wife</em> is very well written, but I hated what this book was doing to me. The WWII in China is merely a backdrop for the protagonist's personal drama of epic proportions; suffice to say that when something very bad, but not exactly cruel, happened <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[(one of protagonist's children dies, quite straightforwardly, of plague) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, I felt relief</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4856634951032306092">I officially do not want to read anything by Tan again. At least this is how I feel at the moment.<br /><br />Why the three stars: <em>The Kitchen God's Wife</em> is very well written, but I hated what this book was doing to me. The WWII in China is merely a backdrop for the protagonist's personal drama of epic proportions; suffice to say that when something very bad, but not exactly cruel, happened <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[(one of protagonist's children dies, quite straightforwardly, of plague) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, I felt relief.<br /><br />Winnie (the protagonist's new, American name) was married to a psychopath. Throughout the book, she has to cope with humiliation, degradation, and a plethora of personal tragedies <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[: she is repeatedly, regularly raped, humiliated by her husband in private and in front of their friends, loses children because of her husband's violence and cruelty - directly and indirectly <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. This is, I think, a book about comradeship and despair, loss and strength; but if you do not bear reading about serious abuse too well, consider skipping this one.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4856634951032306092">...more</a>

  <a href="/user/show/6450088-tania" class="left imgcol" title="Tania"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451883358p2/6450088.jpg" alt="Tania" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/720965908?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 12, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6450088-tania" class="user" title="Tania">Tania</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=favorite-books" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer720965908">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16747100442785612666"><blockquote>She is not related by blood, not even by marriage. She is not someone I chose as my friend. Sometimes I do not even enjoy her company. I do not agree with her opinions. I do not admire her character. And yet we are closer perhaps than sisters, related by fate, joined by debts. I have kept her secrets. She has kept mine. And we have a kind of loyalty that has no word in this country.</blockquote><br /><br />3.5 stars. The Kitchen God's Wife covers many themes and places, yet it is very easy to read. After reading it I ha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16747100442785612666"><blockquote>She is not related by blood, not even by marriage. She is not someone I chose as my friend. Sometimes I do not even enjoy her company. I do not agree with her opinions. I do not admire her character. And yet we are closer perhaps than sisters, related by fate, joined by debts. I have kept her secrets. She has kept mine. And we have a kind of loyalty that has no word in this country.</blockquote><br /><br />3.5 stars. The Kitchen God's Wife covers many themes and places, yet it is very easy to read. After reading it I have a much better understanding of the Chinese culture. Most of the book (all the chapters narrated by Winnie) almost feels like a letter, as there is no dialogue. I thought it would bother me, but I actually enjoyed it. It allowed Winnie to tell her story in her own words, as well as sharing Helen's opinion with us, which showed us what other people thought was going on. And as we know what we feel and experience internally, is not necessarily what other people see. What I loved most was the genuine female characters and the relationships they build. You don't have to always like someone to love them. I will be reading more books by this author.<br /><br /><br />The Story: Pearl Louie Brandt has a terrible secret which she tries desperately to keep from her mother, Winne Louie. And Winnie has long kept her own secrets - about her past and the confusing circumstances of Pearl's birth. Fate intervenes in the form of Helen Kwong, Winnie's so-called sister-in-law, who believes she is dying and must unburden herself of all falsehoods before she flies off to heaven. Thus begins a story that takes us back to Shanghai in the 1920s, through World War II, and the harrowing events that led to Winnie's arrival in America in 1949.<br /><br /><br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_107783086">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107783086" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2087548-kasia-s" class="left imgcol" title="Kasia S."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448424958p2/2087548.jpg" alt="Kasia S." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/107783086?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 19, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2087548-kasia-s" class="user" title="Kasia S.">Kasia S.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer107783086">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14210132265025918273">I adore the way Amy Tan intertwines more than one story line into her books, at first glance it seems that the tale centers on Pearl, the daughter of a Chinese immigrant, who has morphed into the modern American culture and who finds her mother annoying and old fashioned at times. Once the reader gets familiar with Pearl the story then turns back to her mother, Winnie and her childhood friend Helen. Winnie's story is sad and beautiful at the same time, her suffering and struggles to overcome an</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14210132265025918273">I adore the way Amy Tan intertwines more than one story line into her books, at first glance it seems that the tale centers on Pearl, the daughter of a Chinese immigrant, who has morphed into the modern American culture and who finds her mother annoying and old fashioned at times. Once the reader gets familiar with Pearl the story then turns back to her mother, Winnie and her childhood friend Helen. Winnie's story is sad and beautiful at the same time, her suffering and struggles to overcome an abusive husband who's been keeping her from freedom half her life are intense and emotionally moving. Tan's rich, descriptive writing has deep meanings hidden in words. I found myself laughing quite often, which was a surprise because the story is pretty intense. As usual the author supplies us with deep insight into the ugly reality of life, one of my favorite lines was on page 352, "The society is like bright pain applied on top of a rotten wood" which made me stop and think, digest and absorb her wise words, Tan is a master of writing tales with imperfect characters, so many of them have so called rotten bases, and their struggle to improve and move on make the tale even more vivid and intense. In this case it was the way of life for Pearl in wartime China, the harsh reality was that she didn't have much of a saying; all the older men and women in her house seemed to run her life, and the male dominated culture didn't help when the girl was going through hardship, if anything it made her life more hellish, and at times it was hard to read but I continued, good books aren't always pretty.<br /><br />This was a good and potent read, I must warn readers that they might get angry at the bad men in Pearl's life, but her struggles never diminished her personality and her big heart, which she has to this day. I feel that Tan's books not only entertain but also teach a lot, not to mention show us how life in the past was so much harsher, and remind us of individual struggles that women still have to go through, whether they are someone's wife or daughter or best friend, and that deep down we are strong, and our stories are beautiful, and that life might never be fair, but we try our best to fight for it.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14210132265025918273">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1361827299">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16060750269854021692">Pearl Brandt's relationship with her mother is a difficult one; she deplores her mother Winnie's petty criticisms, her bossiness, her superstitious rituals to ward off bad luck, and her negative outlook, so naturally, she is reluctant to tell her mother about her diagnosis of MS. But when her Aunt Helen believes she is dying from a brain tumor, she insists that Pearl tell her mother the truth about her illness, and Helen also pressures Winnie into telling her daughter the truth about her past.<br /><br /><br />Th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16060750269854021692">Pearl Brandt's relationship with her mother is a difficult one; she deplores her mother Winnie's petty criticisms, her bossiness, her superstitious rituals to ward off bad luck, and her negative outlook, so naturally, she is reluctant to tell her mother about her diagnosis of MS. But when her Aunt Helen believes she is dying from a brain tumor, she insists that Pearl tell her mother the truth about her illness, and Helen also pressures Winnie into telling her daughter the truth about her past.<br /><br /><br />Thus begins Winnie's story as a young girl growing up in China, then entering an abusive marriage, suffering the loss of three babies, and living through the horrors and privations of World War 2. We learn how she met Pearl's father and how she came to live in America shortly after the war, where she builds a new life while concealing her past.<br /><br /><br />This was a mesmerizing story about the relationships between friends, and mothers and daughters, a life in prewar and wartime China. It starts out a little slow at first, but once the story of Winnie's past begins to unfold, it is a hard book to put down.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16060750269854021692">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2921601161108845216">I decided to re-read this since it had been at least 15 years since I first read it and I remembered it not one whit (that says more about me than it does the novel). Yet there are Cliffs Notes on it now?! Arrgh! A friend of mine who teaches with me also admitted that she got tired of teaching the Joy Luck Club, so she started teaching this one instead because many of the same themes are explored.<br /><br />I'd agree it's every bit as satisfying as the Joy Luck Club, although if I had to choose between the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2921601161108845216">I decided to re-read this since it had been at least 15 years since I first read it and I remembered it not one whit (that says more about me than it does the novel). Yet there are Cliffs Notes on it now?! Arrgh! A friend of mine who teaches with me also admitted that she got tired of teaching the Joy Luck Club, so she started teaching this one instead because many of the same themes are explored.<br /><br />I'd agree it's every bit as satisfying as the Joy Luck Club, although if I had to choose between the two books to be stranded with on a desert island, I'd pick Amy Tan's first novel. I happen to love the alternating viewpoints in that book (which, I fully realize, confused the heck out of some people). The Kitchen God's Wife has a simple framing device and then it's told mostly from a single point of view, so it's less challenging in that regard. But the mother's story is every bit as heart-wrenching and Tan's pen is every bit as talented here. <br /><br />In fact, Amy Tan has written nothing I didn't like EXCEPT for her last novel, Saving Fish From Drowning, which was a total disaster. Hated. It.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2921601161108845216">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/787480777" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/26521814-anthony-padua" class="left imgcol" title="Anthony Padua"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1387428565p2/26521814.jpg" alt="Anthony Padua" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/787480777?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 08, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/26521814-anthony-padua" class="user" title="Anthony Padua">Anthony Padua</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer787480777">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9180391785292166465">Anthony Padua<br />12-19-13 <br />Per. 3<br />Mr. Brandt Final <br /><br /> The Kitchen God's Wife tells the story of Pearl, a Chinese American woman who lives in California with her family. The book is set in various places in California during the 1980's, when many people from all over the world were immigrating to the U.S. Living with her husband and two daughters, Pearl frequently visits her mother, the very mother who escaped China during the Japanese invasion during World War II. Pearl, as well as her mother does every</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9180391785292166465">Anthony Padua<br />12-19-13 <br />Per. 3<br />Mr. Brandt Final <br /><br /> The Kitchen God's Wife tells the story of Pearl, a Chinese American woman who lives in California with her family. The book is set in various places in California during the 1980's, when many people from all over the world were immigrating to the U.S. Living with her husband and two daughters, Pearl frequently visits her mother, the very mother who escaped China during the Japanese invasion during World War II. Pearl, as well as her mother does everything in their power to ensure a brighter future for their children: running a self sufficient business, telling stories of their past, and spending time as a family. The book is a reminder that family bonds can live even during the toughest of times, which many families need even to this day.<br /><br /> For me, the most memorable moments while reading this book would be when Pearl talks to her mother throughout the book. Their conversations amused me since her mom is quite similar to mine. I wondered if this was casual for Asian parents or if it was a simple coincidence. Even though they love each other, they always seemed to talk in a "playful argument." Her mom would start a conversation by yelling, just like my mom! This made my reading very memorable so far since I was reading something that I myself experience in real life.<br /><br /> Ultimately, the story of Winnie is a story of a Chinese woman starting a new life in America, raising a daughter, and working together as a family. It all adds up to a tale of letting go to the chains that bind us to our past, and example to us all that it is never too late to strive for a better future. The Kitchen God's Wife tells this story very skillfully, reminding us of the struggles of life of an ordinary Chinese immigrant, living first in China, then in the United States.<br /><br /> I would personally recommend this book to people who had ancestors who lived in Asia, but moved to a new home like in the United States. Reading this book was almost like looking into my roots of why I was born here instead of the Philippines. The Japanese invasion of Southeast Asia is the reason why Filipinos and Chinese alike moved away from their beloved homeland. With war aside along with any other reasons, the struggles are very similar. That is why I recommend this book to people who want an insight into their hidden past.<br /><br />(Side note: All people living in the U.S. are either an immigrant, or a descendent of an immigrant!)<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9180391785292166465">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13149369546486076950">This book was better than expected. I especially loved the relationship between Winnie and Helen. The relationship reminded me of my Mom's with her sister with some crazy ways of looking at things. <br /><br />Winnie went through some hard, terrible times. Yet the book was not depressing. Winnie had a lot of inner strength. She just didn't know it and thus she lacked confidence in herself. Plus she was also trapped. Living with fear and brutality really impacts one's mind to the point where just surviving is major and Winnie was a survivor. <br /><br />The relationships that she had along her journey were indeed special. There were secrets and hiding of sorrow and shame, but there was also a closeness and a certain bonding that perhaps only women could understand. <br /><br />It was enjoyable to learn more about China and its customs.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9139382615570916917">Another fantastic work by Tan. Tan has a tremendous gift when it comes to her story-telling and her ability to interweave Chinese culture, language and history is not only genius, but also gives her works of fiction incredible authenticity.<br /><br />TKGW is a story within a story about three Chinese women who emigrated to America, but the real story (and the better story) is what happened to them before they came to America - WWII, the men in their lives and their children not only define the women that t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9139382615570916917">Another fantastic work by Tan. Tan has a tremendous gift when it comes to her story-telling and her ability to interweave Chinese culture, language and history is not only genius, but also gives her works of fiction incredible authenticity.<br /><br />TKGW is a story within a story about three Chinese women who emigrated to America, but the real story (and the better story) is what happened to them before they came to America - WWII, the men in their lives and their children not only define the women that they became, but provides great understanding in the development of their friendship and loyalty to each other.<br /><br />A wonderful read that I highly recommend, especially if the reader has read and enjoyed <a title="The Joy Luck Club by Amy Tan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7763.The_Joy_Luck_Club">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="The Joy Luck Club by Amy Tan" src="http://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/books/1255589685s/7763.jpg" /></a>.<br /><br /></span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13595875046302139363">I thought I had read this book many years ago, but knew that if I had, I would notice right away. I guess I didn't read it! What an wonderful story. A story of secrets held for many many years. Of a Mother and daughter from not only different generations, but cultures and continents. I find the stories of new immigrants and their American born children fascinating. Particularly when the immigrants life in their home country was impoverished, abusive, and horrifying particularly due to war. There is then such a huge gap between the parents and children, and lack of understanding, unless stories are shared. The threat given, of secrets being exposed, sets the story up for a history that the mother shares with her daughter. It's never too late to reveal how your history impacted on who you are today. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2357738484301256919">I wasn't quite expecting this book to rival Joy Luck Club in complexity or originality, but I don't think it even came close. It starts out with a mother/daughter relationship, but the story quickly turns to the mother's story of an abusive first marriage in feudal China and WWI. The characters are one dimensional and the story is just not original. Her husband is just absurdly bad and most of the characters are flat and uninteresting. It's too long for what it wanted to convey. At the end, it t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2357738484301256919">I wasn't quite expecting this book to rival Joy Luck Club in complexity or originality, but I don't think it even came close. It starts out with a mother/daughter relationship, but the story quickly turns to the mother's story of an abusive first marriage in feudal China and WWI. The characters are one dimensional and the story is just not original. Her husband is just absurdly bad and most of the characters are flat and uninteresting. It's too long for what it wanted to convey. At the end, it tries to tie back into the mother/daughter relationship, but just falls flat. It's just a typical woman beating the odds and finding true love story that just happens to be set in China. There is very little emotional depth or even cultural insight. </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10684626-lynn?shelf=cross-cultural" class="actionLinkLite">cross-cultural</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10684626-lynn?shelf=own-it" class="actionLinkLite">own-it</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10684626-lynn?shelf=china" class="actionLinkLite">china</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10684626-lynn?shelf=literary-wives-online-book-discussi" class="actionLinkLite">literary-wives-online-book-discussi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10684626-lynn?shelf=literary-wives-theme" class="actionLinkLite">literary-wives-theme</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer613445368">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13643933252070467741">Full review: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://books-n-music.blogspot.com/2015/12/literary-wives-18_7.html" target="_blank">http://books-n-music.blogspot.com/201...</a>. I never cease to be amazed at the treatment and plight of women throughout history. I'm certain I wouldn't have lived long, 'cause, honestly, my goal would have been to make sure I took out at least a couple of the meanest men with me! Unbelievable to me and so very very sad. How can dismissing half the human race be justified? I assume Tan is accurate in her portrayal of females in China during the early to almost mid-20th Century. And if s</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13643933252070467741">Full review: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://books-n-music.blogspot.com/2015/12/literary-wives-18_7.html" target="_blank">http://books-n-music.blogspot.com/201...</a>. I never cease to be amazed at the treatment and plight of women throughout history. I'm certain I wouldn't have lived long, 'cause, honestly, my goal would have been to make sure I took out at least a couple of the meanest men with me! Unbelievable to me and so very very sad. How can dismissing half the human race be justified? I assume Tan is accurate in her portrayal of females in China during the early to almost mid-20th Century. And if so...well, it just wasn't right. And her first/Chinese husband. What a vile creature. Wen Fu had absolutely no redeeming qualities whatsoever. I was so very glad for her to find Jimmie and make a life with him in the U.S. At least she was able to enjoy what appeared to be a true partnership and genuine love and respect with her second husband. (I feel as if I know that feeling well!) And the keeping of secrets and divulging of withheld knowledge and history...such a fascinating practice amongst us humans toward one another! Especially with regard to our own children. More and more I realize that no one person knows every little thing about any of us. We choose to share certain things with certain others, but I'm sure no one person has shared every little thing about themselves with any one other person...we spread ourselves out amongst those we know, as it were. And really, what do our grown children know about us? Especially with regard to our prior history before raising children? Probably very little. And if they knew more? Would it be eye-opening as it seemed to be for Pearl? Or not...? What an amazing study of secrets and relationships!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13643933252070467741">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">teenagers </span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer42863224">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4886971485891859799">I have to say, this book probably took me more than 2 months to finish. At first I regretted picking and buying this book when I read the first 200 pages. But when I was told we had 3 more weeks before the marking period ended, I panicked! This was the only book that I read throughout the whole marking period for advisory and I still have about 200 more pages to go. So then I forced myself to read at least 10 minutes per night. Then on Friday night (1/9), I got hooked onto this book. I read at l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4886971485891859799">I have to say, this book probably took me more than 2 months to finish. At first I regretted picking and buying this book when I read the first 200 pages. But when I was told we had 3 more weeks before the marking period ended, I panicked! This was the only book that I read throughout the whole marking period for advisory and I still have about 200 more pages to go. So then I forced myself to read at least 10 minutes per night. Then on Friday night (1/9), I got hooked onto this book. I read at least 40 pages that night. Then on Saturday night, instead of doing my homework, I was reading my book. Then on Sunday, having only 6 hours of sleep, I woke up to finish up my book! <br /><br />After finishing this, I definitely didn't regret getting this book at all! In fact, I love this book and I plan to get more books written by Amy Tan. I definitely learned a lot from “The Kitchen God’s Wife”. It was about a mother, Winnie, who was telling her daughter, Pearl, about her life (which she kept a secret of) in China before she came to America. She went through unfortunate events that all place in the early 1900s which was the time when Japan was trying to take over China and the Communist starting to take over. Her mother left her when she was young then her father made her move to her aunt’s place. Afterwards she got married to a very bad man that abused her and forced her to have sex with him and sadly, the babies that she had with him all died. But later, she runs away with the man she falls in love with and marries to come to America. <br /><br />I definitely cried reading the last half of the book since it was more focused on her in China. In the beginning, the first few chapters were about her daughter and her family in America. Then later, it led to the Winnie having her Pearl sit down and listen to her talk about her wretched life in China and who her real father was. After telling her about life, her daughter decided to tell her mother her secret that she kept from her. <br /><br />I felt really happy with what I have now after reading this book. What Winnie went through in China was misery. Like her mom leaving her when she was young and never had her father’s love and getting married with someone she didn’t know and getting abused afterwards. She had no choice and couldn’t change what she had until she fled. For me, I my parents love and they would force to me make choices that would make my future miserable. That’s why I feel really grateful afterwards. <br /><br />What I learned from this is to take a chance. In the end, Winnie ran away from her husband for the man that she loved. Though she was thrown in jail later, she still made it through and got married to him. She took a chance to make a change. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4886971485891859799">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8970425844458807251">I'm starting to become a big fan of Asian themed novels. These books give you a sneak peek into the their world and family relationships. Most of Tan's novel is based on relationships between mothers and daughters in Asian families. In this novel, Winnie, the mother of Pearl has a deep dark secret that she's been keeping to herself for years. It isn't until someone close to her decides to unleash that secret that Winnie decides its time to reveal the truth to her daughter. <br /><br />No matter whether your</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8970425844458807251">I'm starting to become a big fan of Asian themed novels. These books give you a sneak peek into the their world and family relationships. Most of Tan's novel is based on relationships between mothers and daughters in Asian families. In this novel, Winnie, the mother of Pearl has a deep dark secret that she's been keeping to herself for years. It isn't until someone close to her decides to unleash that secret that Winnie decides its time to reveal the truth to her daughter. <br /><br />No matter whether your black, white, or hispanic, you can relate to the relationship between Winnie and Pearl. And women from all cultures can go through devestating things that have an impact on their life. <br /><br />The book is also a testament to a someone's will and determination to come through a damaging situation. <br /><br />Tan is an incredible writer who can get to the heart of a character. I truly enjoyed this book. </span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14616559071284724425">This was my first Tan novel. Previously I read her memoir on writing, The Opposite of Fate, and I loved it. I figured I'd read her fiction when the right time came and the right time came when I moved to China and had no internet. <br /><br />Wow, so what to say? This book contains a whole lot of sad. Is that Tan's thing? I'm not sure but it IS a really beautiful portrayal of mother/daughter relationships, and all sorts of family and friendship relationships. And the plot twists, gosh the plot twists! Tan is good at plot twists and at making you cringe and she should get a deal with Trojan because she'll really make you think twice about unprotected sex. <br /><br />I finished the book a matter of weeks ago and I wish I had more to say, but I don't. Pick it up, it's my first but I hear it's one of her best. Once I read more by this author, I'll let you know if that's true. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_976407814">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/976407814" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6948752-barbara" class="left imgcol" title="Barbara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1401319183p2/6948752.jpg" alt="Barbara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/976407814?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6948752-barbara" class="user" title="Barbara">Barbara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer976407814">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5996370596129727031">This a story about family. Set mostly in China during WWII, Winnie attempts to describe her life in China to her daughter. This is a family with many secrets. Truly, this is a story of fear, guilt, obligation, forgiveness, redemption, but most of all, it is a story about love. <br /><br />Be prepared, this book will make you hungry. There are multiple descriptions of food. The whole story is a sensory adventure with all the smells and sounds and tastes vividly described. <br /><br />This book will make you cry. The cha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5996370596129727031">This a story about family. Set mostly in China during WWII, Winnie attempts to describe her life in China to her daughter. This is a family with many secrets. Truly, this is a story of fear, guilt, obligation, forgiveness, redemption, but most of all, it is a story about love. <br /><br />Be prepared, this book will make you hungry. There are multiple descriptions of food. The whole story is a sensory adventure with all the smells and sounds and tastes vividly described. <br /><br />This book will make you cry. The characters are interesting, even though you make not like most of them very much. Some of them you may identify with, or know someone very like them. Isn't this the way friendship is? No one is perfect and sometimes our friends get on our nerves and we often disagree with them, but the shared experiences of our lives are often what bind us most closely with those who are our "friends" even more so than our actual family members.<br /><br />Well worth reading, if only for the insiders view of Chinese culture of the 1930's and 40's.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5996370596129727031">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/5868516-natalie" class="left imgcol" title="Natalie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446087304p2/5868516.jpg" alt="Natalie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/306433477?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 05, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5868516-natalie" class="user" title="Natalie">Natalie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer306433477">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2123100855025101928">I lost momentum about halfway through this book. The beginning was interesting, with the dynamic between the mother and daughter. I hate to say it, but I actually grew tired of reading about how crappy her life was. I know it sounds callous, but I have no respect for a woman who stays with her husband after he maims and then kills her child. Had that been me, I would have gone anywhere, even lived off the streets to get away from that man. The problem was, Weila was so proud and didn't want to l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2123100855025101928">I lost momentum about halfway through this book. The beginning was interesting, with the dynamic between the mother and daughter. I hate to say it, but I actually grew tired of reading about how crappy her life was. I know it sounds callous, but I have no respect for a woman who stays with her husband after he maims and then kills her child. Had that been me, I would have gone anywhere, even lived off the streets to get away from that man. The problem was, Weila was so proud and didn't want to lose face or make her family look bad. I can't feel bad for anything that happened to her past that point, because she made poor choices and stayed with Wen Fu. Anyway, it was an okay story and I finally pushed through it. I would like an update as to how the characters are doing now, because this story was written in 1991. Are Pearl and Winnie and the rest still alive? What are Pearl's children doing now? I'd love an update, as the story ends before really resolving the present day problems. Not sure I'd read this one again, as it's way too frustrating.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2123100855025101928">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_341348609">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/341348609" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6330462-hildred-billings" class="left imgcol" title="Hildred Billings"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442644055p2/6330462.jpg" alt="Hildred Billings" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/341348609?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 02, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6330462-hildred-billings" class="user" title="Hildred Billings">Hildred Billings</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer341348609">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer908579067433699780">If there's one thing Amy Tan is comfortable with, it's the mother-daughter relationship, particularly between Chinese immigrant mothers and their jaded American-born daughters. "The Kitchen God's Wife" is absolutely no exception. Tan's sophomore novel, "The Kitchen God's Wife" follows on the footstep's of Tan's smash debut "The Joy Luck Club". The easiest way to look at this novel is as essentially the same thing as "The Joy Luck Club", but focusing on one relationship as opposed to four. <br /><br />The st</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText908579067433699780">If there's one thing Amy Tan is comfortable with, it's the mother-daughter relationship, particularly between Chinese immigrant mothers and their jaded American-born daughters. "The Kitchen God's Wife" is absolutely no exception. Tan's sophomore novel, "The Kitchen God's Wife" follows on the footstep's of Tan's smash debut "The Joy Luck Club". The easiest way to look at this novel is as essentially the same thing as "The Joy Luck Club", but focusing on one relationship as opposed to four. <br /><br />The story opens with Pearl, a married-with-two-kids woman approaching middle age and discovering she has MS. Everyone around her knows of her MS except for her mother, Winnie. While Pearl ho-hums over whether or not to tell her mother, Winnie's "best friend", Helen, drops the bomb that she'll tell Winnie if Pearl doesn't. Likewise, Helen threatens Winnie that she'll tell Pearl about <em>Winnie's</em> secrets. Of course, this is all done under a thin guise of Helen pretending to have a brain tumor.<br /><br />When then happens is that Winnie comes completely clean about her life. The bulk of the story is a framed "story within a story" as Winnie gives her daughter a first hand account of youth and life in pre-Communist China. If you've read "The Joy Luck Club", you can guess about how cheery and delightful it is.<br /><br />Winnie's horror story of being abandoned by her mother, cast out by her father, married to one of the most evil men to ever grace literature, and one dead baby after another....all while the Japanese are dropping bombs and the Kuomintang take no prisoners...is enough to put anyone with even a hard stomach off. But if there's another thing Tan is great at, it's her voice. The story is told in a very easy conversational style, full of Winnie's random observations, funny digressions, and always laced in the hope that kept her going. Winnie always declares herself as "weak", but anyone reading her story can safely say she's one of the strongest characters to ever survive an Amy Tan plot.<br /><br />That all said, there are a couple things keeping this book from reaching five glorious stars. First, is the obvious reuse of a story type already seen 4-8 times over in "The Joy Luck Club". The second is a complete lack of empathy for Pearl and where her story is even going. She spends most of her three chapters she gets whining to herself, about her mother, about how she's the queen of passive aggression to everyone around her. Also, as somebody with a mother who has MS, and while I understand this was written over twenty years ago, good Lord. Your life does not revolve around your MS.<br /><br />My last great gripe is the title and its relevance to the plot and to the characters. All right, that's hyperbole. It does have a place. But it's completely glossed over and relegated to one paragraph towards the beginning of the book. The whole deal with the "Kitchen God" feels like an afterthought on Tan's part. I get the relation of the title to Winnie, the main character, but damn if I didn't care most of the time.<br /><br />But overall, it's another great piece of fiction and storytelling from one of my favorite authors. And like all her stories, it makes you question your relationship with your family and think "what if" when it comes to wondering if your mother is holding some great secret from you. But as I mentioned before, my mother has MS, like Pearl, and at this point she's probably forgotten any of the great secrets she may have been harboring inside.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="908579067433699780">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_81009279">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81009279" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/56729-kathryn" class="left imgcol" title="Kathryn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1176337440p2/56729.jpg" alt="Kathryn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/81009279?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 14, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/56729-kathryn" class="user" title="Kathryn">Kathryn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12557.The_Kitchen_God_s_Wife" title="Paperback 9780143038108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer81009279'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer81009279">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10601619853625555869">On the whole, I really enjoyed this book. As a third-generation ABC (American-born Chinese), I love reading Amy Tan's books because I always discover in reading them that something my family has always done or ways we've always thought is Chinese in nature, not universal. This book focused less on a mother-daughter theme (despite the framework of a mother telling her daughter her life story) and puts more of its efforts into painting a picture of Chinese life, thinking, and experiences during WW</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10601619853625555869">On the whole, I really enjoyed this book. As a third-generation ABC (American-born Chinese), I love reading Amy Tan's books because I always discover in reading them that something my family has always done or ways we've always thought is Chinese in nature, not universal. This book focused less on a mother-daughter theme (despite the framework of a mother telling her daughter her life story) and puts more of its efforts into painting a picture of Chinese life, thinking, and experiences during WWII.<br /><br />I think Tan did an excellent job with Winnie's life narrative, but I did feel at the end of the book that she could have done a little more to wrap up the inner story and to make the outer story more complete. I felt that Winnie's story ended abruptly with Wen Fu's last visit. I would have liked to read more about her journey to the US and how she and Jimmy Louie started their life here in the US, how she suppressed her secret that Pearl's father was not Jimmy, and how that affected their relationship. I also thought there was an imbalance--at the beginning of the book, Tan devoted a good amount of pages to setting up the framework, but at the end so few. I would have liked to read more about how Pearl felt about what her mom told her. (Not to mention her reaction was way less emotional than I'd expect...) It would have also been interesting to see how she brought this information back to her husband, how he'd react.<br /><br />Overall, it was an enjoyable, thought-provoking read, but the book ended a little too abruptly for my liking. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10601619853625555869">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cKyuIFeGXBEAXuBnJSbKX74+HkhR5f56zqA+/cx5k4AVeJHzpVRI64yGlNxbRyzt98ndURg4udCkqTtvAOxlxQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('l4RHe4DE9wJTJT+V7+4j+dGKKS3BBbSdeBfq7EWe5mDyUHiochbj+N/9Sy6Rj8VLmH3qNIjY8zcSHu9+iQsQJQ==')})
  }

These memoirs of the author\'s first twenty-four years are thoughtful, informative, and highly entertaining. They not only portray a young woman and her unique family in San Francisco\'s Chinatown, but they are rich in the details that light up a world within the world of America. The third-person singular style is rooted in Chinese literary form, reflecting cultural disregard for the individual, yet Jad Snow Wong\'s story also is typically American.\n\nWe first meet Jade Snow Wong the child, narrowly confined by the family and factory life, bound to respect and obey her elders while shouldering responsibility for younger brothers and sisters - a solemn child well versed in the proper order of things, who knew that punishment was sure for any infraction of etiquette. Then the schoolgirl caught in confusion between the rigid teaching of her ancestors and the strange ways of her foreign classmates. After that the college student feeling her was toward personal identity in the face of parental indifference or outright opposition. And finally the artist whose early triumphs were doubled by the knowledge that she had at long last won recognition from her family.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"15688901406987542972\" She quickly learns that in traditional Kyoto, personal boundaries are firmly drawn and actions are not always what they appear.\n\nIn the midst of her acculturation, Sarah learns of a family secret. During World War II, her grandmother was forced to give up one of her daughters for adoption. The child was adopted by the grandmother\'s sister-in-law, and the siblings were brought up as cousins, growing up on the same lane where both the biological and adoptive mother lived. Even into the present, the arrangement is never discussed. But as Sarah learns, its presence looms over the two houses. In this carefully articulated world, where every gesture and look has meaning, Sarah must learn the rules by which her mother, aunts, and grandmother live.\n\nDelicately balancing drama and restraint Inside the Forbidden City, she witnessed seductions, plots, murders, and brazen acts of treason. Propelled by a shrewd intelligence, an extraordinary persistence, and a friendship with the imperial heir, she rose through the ranks to become the first Empress of China. On the one hand, she was a political mastermind who quelled insurrections, eased famine, and opened wide the routes of international trade. On the other, she was a passionate patron of the arts who brought Chinese civilization to unsurpassed heights of knowledge, beauty, and sophistication.\n\nAnd yet, from the moment of her death to the present day, her name has been sullied, her story distorted, and her memoirs obliterated by men taking vengeance on a women who dared become Emperor. For the first time in thirteen centuries, Empress Wu flings open the gates of her Forbidden City and tells her own astonishing tale–revealing a fascinating, complex figure who in many ways remains A true believer--and one of only two Westerners permitted to enroll at Beijing University--her education included wielding a pneumatic drill at the Number One Machine Tool Factory. In the name of the Revolution, she renounced rock &amp;amp; roll, hauled pig manure in the paddy fields, and turned in a fellow student who sought her help in getting to the United States. She also met and married the only American draft dodger from the Vietnam War to seek asylum in China.Red China Blues is Wong\'s startling--and ironic--memoir of her rocky six-year romance with Maoism (which crumbled as she became aware of the harsh realities of Chinese communism); her dramatic firsthand account of the devastating Tiananmen Square uprising; and her engaging portrait of the individuals and events she covered as a correspondent in China during the tumultuous era of capitalist reform under Deng Xiaoping. In a frank, captivating, deeply personal narrative she relates the horrors that led to her disillusionment with the &amp;quot;worker\'s paradise.&amp;quot; And through the stories of the people--an unhappy young woman who was sold into marriage, China\'s most famous dissident, a doctor who lengthens penises--Wong reveals long-hidden dimensions of the world\'s most populous nation.In setting out to show readers in the Western world what life is like in China, and why we should care, she reacquaints herself with the old friends--and enemies of her radical past, and comes to terms with the     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5246.Amy_Tan">About Amy Tan</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/5246.Amy_Tan"><img alt="5246" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1437607346p2/5246.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer10410965199670676685">Amy Tan (Chinese: ???; pinyin: Tán ?nm?i; born February 19, 1952) is an American writer whose works explore mother-daughter relationships and what it means to grow up as a first generation Asian American. In 1993, Tan's adaptation of her most popular fiction work, The Joy Luck Club, became a commercially successful film.<br /><br />She has written several other books, including The Kitchen God's Wife, The Hun</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10410965199670676685">Amy Tan (Chinese: ???; pinyin: Tán ?nm?i; born February 19, 1952) is an American writer whose works explore mother-daughter relationships and what it means to grow up as a first generation Asian American. In 1993, Tan's adaptation of her most popular fiction work, The Joy Luck Club, became a commercially successful film.<br /><br />She has written several other books, including The Kitchen God's Wife, The Hundred Secret Senses, and The Bonesetter's Daughter, and a collection of non-fiction essays entitled The Opposite of Fate: A Book of Musings. Her most recent book, Saving Fish From Drowning, explores the tribulations experienced by a group of people who disappear while on an art expedition into the jungles of Burma. In addition, Tan has written two children's books: The Moon Lady (1992) and Sagwa, The Chinese Siamese Cat (1994), which was turned into an animated series airing on PBS. She has also appeared on PBS in a short spot on encouraging children to write.<br /><br />Currently, she is the literary editor for West, Los Angeles Times' Sunday magazine.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10410965199670676685">...more</a>

    </div>
